The Reality and Obligation of Believing in the Recording Angels
Declares the obligation of belief in the literal writing of the recording angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran and hadith affirm a literal writing by the recording angels for the believer's spiritual benefit, and that denying this reality constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Denying the angels' literal writing denies the Quran – belief is obligatory.

The Sunni creed includes belief in the recording angels who write deeds. This lesson establishes that this writing is real, not mere metaphor, and that denying it is disbelief. We will see the scriptural evidence, the spiritual benefit, and the proper attitude of tafwid toward the modality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the author's own statements before any interpretation.
Notice the direct citation of Quran 82:11-12.
2
Identify the ruling: denial is kufr
The author states that denying the recording angels' writing leads to disbelief because it denies the Quran. Reflect on why this is a serious matter.
3
Understand the benefit for the believer
The writing is not for Allah's need but for the servant's benefit – it inspires shame and helps avoid sin. How does this affect your practice?
4
Note the defense of literal writing
The author insists the writing is real, with paper and ink, citing a hadith about the tongue as pen. Contrast this with metaphorical interpretations.
5
Recognize tafwid as the safest approach
The author concludes that delegating the exact modality to Allah (tafwid) is more appropriate, meaning we affirm the reality without speculating how.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الكتابة مما يجب الإيمان بها، فيكفر منكرها لتكذيبه القرآن قال تعالى : وكراما كَذِبِينَ يَعْلَمُونَ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ ﴾ [الانفطار : ۱۱-۱۲]، لكنها ليست لحاجة دعت إليها ؛ وإنما فائدتها أن العبد إذا علم بها استحيا وترك المعصية. والكتب حقيقي بآلة وقرطاس ومداد يعلمها الله سبحانه وتعالى حملاً للنصوص على ظواهرها خلافاً لمن قال : إنه كناية عن الحفظ والعلم، وفي بعض الأحاديث أن لسانه قلمهما وريقه مدادهما، والتفويض أولى.
And this writing is among what it is obligatory to have faith in. Thus, the one who denies it disbelieves due to his denial of the Qur'an. Allah Most High said: 'While honorable scribes write, they know what you do' (Q. 82:11-12). But it is not due to a need that necessitated it; rather, its benefit is that when the servant knows it, he feels shame and leaves disobedience. And the writing is real, with a tool, paper, and ink, which Allah the Exalted knows, as a carrying of the texts upon their apparent meanings, contrary to the one who said: It is a metaphor for preservation and knowledge. And in some hadiths, that his tongue is their pen and his saliva is their ink. And delegation (tafwid) is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a declaration of doctrine regarding the recording angels (kiraman katibin). The author states that belief in this writing is obligatory (wajib al-iman), and that denying it constitutes disbelief (kufr) because it is a denial of the Qur'anic verses that affirm it. He cites the verse from Surat al-Infitar. He then explains that this writing is not due to any need on Allah's part, but its benefit is to make the servant feel shame and abandon sin. The author then defends the literal reality of this writing: it is real writing with a tool, paper, and ink known to Allah, in accordance with the apparent meaning of the texts (zahir al-nusus). This is contrary to those who interpret it metaphorically as mere preservation or knowledge. He supports his view with a hadith stating that the tongue of the person is the pen of the angels and their saliva is the ink. However, he concludes that delegation (tafwid) of the exact modality to Allah is more appropriate, meaning we affirm the reality without speculating on how.

Key Terms
كتابة
ملائكة كرام كاتبون
تكذيب القرآن
ظاهر النصوص
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the writing of the recording angels is purely metaphorical or symbolic, not a real inscription.
Correction: The author affirms it is real (haqiqi) with physical means, following the apparent meaning of texts, though the exact modality is delegated to Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on denying the reality of the recording angels' writing according to the passage?
A) It is a minor sin
B) It is disbelief (kufr) because it denies the Quran
C) It is permissible as long as one believes in the angels
D) It is a matter of interpretation and not a sin

Key Takeaway
Belief in the recording angels' real writing is obligatory; denying it is disbelief, but its modality is entrusted to Allah (tafwid).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every word and every deed is being recorded right now? What sin do you commit secretly that you would be ashamed to see written in your book? Will you repent from it today before it is read to you?
?
The angels write with a tool, paper, and ink known only to Allah—an unseen reality. When you are alone, does this knowledge change your actions? What specific step will you take tonight to remind yourself that your deeds are being written?
?
If denying the reality of the recording angels is a cause of disbelief, have you ever considered this matter lightly or dismissed it as mere metaphor? What will you do now to ensure your belief in this matter is firm and not just a passing thought?

Where Do the Recording Angels Reside? Scholarly Opinions and the Safe Stance
Lists various opinions on where the two angels reside on the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various scholarly opinions on the location of the two recording angels on a person and the author's advice to refrain from speculation.

Core Idea
The safest position on the angels' location is to stop—waqf.

Islamic scholars have offered several opinions about where the two recording angels are stationed on a person's body. Some suggested fixed points like the molars or shoulders, while others proposed they move with the person's posture. This passage surveys these views and concludes with a cautionary note: the safest approach is to refrain from speculating beyond what is revealed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the opinions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. List each location mentioned: molars, shoulders, chin, lips, upper lip, and the dynamic positions based on posture.
Notice the variety: some are fixed body parts, others change with movement.
2
Identify the common thread
Consider whether these opinions share any principle. Most opinions propose a nearby location, but Mujahid's view adds movement. The key is that there is no single agreed-upon location.
3
Understand the author's stance
The author recommends 'waqf'—to stop and not specify. Reflect on why this is considered the safest approach in matters not explicitly detailed in revelation.
This reflects a cautious theological methodology.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Think about the principle of avoiding unnecessary speculation in theology. How does this protect the believer from error?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في محلّهما من الشخص، فقيل : ناجذاه؛ أي : آخر أضراسه الأيمن والأيسر، وقيل عاتقاه وقيل ذقنه وقيل ،شفتاه وقيل : عنفقته، ورُوي عن مجاهد أنه إن قعد كان أحدهما عن يمينه والآخر عن يساره، وإن مشى كان أحدهما أمامه والآخر وراءه، وإن رقد كان أحدهما عند رأسه والآخر عند رجليه، ويجمع بين هذه الأقاويل بأنهما لا يلزمان محلاً واحداً، والأسلم في أمثال ذلك الوقف.
And there is disagreement concerning their place on the person. So it is said: His two molars, i.e., the last of his molars, the right and left. And it is said: His two shoulders. And it is said: His chin. And it is said: His two lips. And it is said: The hair of his upper lip. And it is narrated from Mujahid: When he sits, one of them is on his right and the other on his left; when he walks, one of them is in front of him and the other behind him; when he lies down, one of them is at his head and the other at his feet. And a reconciliation between these statements is made by saying that they do not necessarily adhere to one place. And the safest in such matters is to stop (waqf).
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a survey of scholarly opinions on the physical location of the two recording angels on a person. The author presents several views: some say they are on the molars (the last right and left molars), others say on the shoulders, the chin, the lips, or the hair of the upper lip (ʿunfuqa). He then narrates from Mujahid a more dynamic position: the angels change positions according to the person's posture—when sitting, one is on the right and one on the left; when walking, one in front and one behind; when lying down, one at the head and one at the feet. The author then offers a reconciliation: the angels do not adhere to a single fixed location. He concludes that the safest approach (aslam) in such matters is waqf—i.e., to refrain from speculating and to stop at what the texts say without further elaboration. This reflects a cautious theological methodology.

Key Terms
محل الملكين
ناجذ
عاتق
ذقن
شفة
عنفقة
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is one definitive fixed location for the recording angels on the body.
Correction: The passage shows multiple opinions and the author advises not to specify a location, indicating that this is not a matter of theological certainty.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude is the safest position regarding the location of the two angels?
A) They are on the shoulders
B) They change positions based on posture
C) To stop (waqf) and not specify
D) They are on the molars

Key Takeaway
The safest approach regarding the exact location of the recording angels is to refrain from speculation and adopt the position of waqf (stopping).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your faith have you been tempted to seek details that Allah has not revealed? Will you trust His wisdom and practice waqf, stopping where the revelation stops?
?
The recording angels are constantly with you, yet their exact location remains unknown. How does this certainty of their presence without knowing the specifics affect your awareness of Allah’s watchfulness? Will you let that awareness change a single action today?
?
When you encounter differing scholarly opinions on matters of the unseen, how do you react? Are you impatient for certainty, or can you humbly say 'Allah knows best'? Commit today to adopt the safer stance of waqf in such matters.

Angels Write Every Action, Not Just Speech
Explains that angels write all actions, not just speech, and discusses writing of permissible acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that angels record all human actions, including permissible and disliked acts, and that good and evil deeds are written distinctly.

Core Idea
Angels write everything you do, even the mundane.

In this passage, Sanusi expands on the line 'They will not neglect from his affair anything he did.' He emphasizes that the recording angels do not limit themselves to speech; they write every action. He supports this with Quran and hadith, and then explains how permissible and disliked acts are handled. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the author first explains the line, then provides evidence from Quran and hadith.
2
Understand the scope of writing
Identify the author's claim that writing is not exclusive to words. Look at the examples from the hadith.
3
Examine the handling of permissible and disliked acts
See how the hadith describes the cancellation of mubah and makruh acts, and the different views on whether they are written.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئاً فَعَلْ أي: لن يتركوا من شأنه وحاله شيئاً فعله بلا كتابة بل يكتبونه قولاً أو غيره، فليست الكتابة مختصة بالأقوال، وإن كان قوله تعالى: ﴿مَا يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَيدٌ﴾ [ق: ۱۸] في خصوص الأقوال، وكذا حديث ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما في تفسير الآية المذكورة ) ، فإنه قال : يكتب كل ما يتكلم به من خير أو شر حتى إنه ليكتب قوله : أكلت شربت، ذهبت، جنت، رأيت، حتى إذا كان الخميس ويوم الاثنين عُرض قوله وعمله، فأقرَّ منه ما كان يوم خيراً أو شرًا وألغي سائره؛ أي باقيه وهو المباح والمكروه، فتلتقمه حيتان البحر فتموت منه لنتنه ، فتخرج منه دود يأكل الزرع، وهذا صريح في كتب المباحات، فيؤيد القول بكتابتها، لكن تقدَّم أن بعضهم اعتمد عدم كتابتها، وظواهر الآثار أن الحسنات تكتب مميزة عن السيئات فقيل : إن سيئات المؤمن أول كتابه، وآخره هذه الذنوب قد سترتها ،وغفرتها، وحسنات الكافر أول كتابه وآخره هذه حسناتك قد رددتها عليك وما قبلتها . قوله : ( ولو ذَهِلٌ) أي : ولو غَفَل ونسي ؛ فالذهول عن الشيء نسيانه والغفلة عنه، فيكتب ما فعله نسياناً، وإن كان لا يؤاخذ به ؛ لأنَّه ليس الغرض من الكتابة المعاقبة، ولا الإثابة.
His statement: 'They will not neglect from his affair anything he did' means: they will not leave out of his state and condition anything he did without writing; rather, they write it, whether speech or otherwise. So writing is not exclusive to words, even though Allah's statement: 'Not a word does he utter but there is with him a watcher, ready' (Q. 50:18) is specific to words. Likewise, the hadith of Ibn Abbas in the interpretation of the mentioned verse: He said: He writes everything that he speaks of good or evil, until he writes his saying: I ate, I drank, I went, I came, I saw. Then on Thursday and Monday, his speech and deed are presented, and what was good or evil is confirmed, and the remainder is cancelled; i.e., the rest of it, which is the permissible (mubah) and the disliked (makruh). Then fish of the sea swallow it and die from its foulness, then worms come out of it that eat the crops. And this is explicit about the writing of permissible acts, so it supports the opinion of writing them. But it has preceded that some relied on not writing them. And the apparent meanings of the traditions are that good deeds are written distinct from evil deeds, so it is said: Indeed, the sins of the believer are the first of his book, and its end: 'These sins I have veiled and forgiven.' And the good deeds of the disbeliever are the first of his book and its end: 'These good deeds I have returned to you and not accepted them.' His statement: 'And even if forgetful' means: even if he is heedless and forgetful; for heedlessness of something is its forgetfulness and neglect. So he writes what he did forgetfully, even if he is not punished for it, because the purpose of writing is not punishment nor reward.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on two lines from the poem: 'They will not neglect from his affair anything he did' and 'And even if forgetful.' The author explains the first line by clarifying that the angels write everything a person does, not just speech, even though the verse in Surat Qaf specifically mentions words. He then cites a hadith from Ibn Abbas that expands on this: the angels write every utterance, even mundane things like 'I ate, I drank,' etc. Then, on Mondays and Thursdays, deeds are presented; good and evil deeds are confirmed, but permissible (mubah) and disliked (makruh) acts are cancelled and given to fish that die from their foulness. This supports the view that permissible acts are written, though some scholars hold they are not written. The author then notes that traditions indicate good and evil deeds are recorded distinctly, and he mentions an interpretation: for the believer, sins are written first but then forgiven; for the disbeliever, good deeds are written first but then rejected. Finally, he explains the second line: 'even if forgetful' means that even if one acts forgetfully, the angel writes it, but the person is not held accountable because the purpose of writing is not punishment or reward but documentation.

Key Terms
لن يهملوا
مباح
مكروه
عرض الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels only record speech.
Correction: The author shows that angels record all actions, not just speech.

Checkpoint

A) They are confirmed
B) They are cancelled and eaten by fish
C) They are shown to the person
D) They are converted into good deeds

Key Takeaway
Angels record every action, including permissible acts, but accountability varies; good and evil are written distinctly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage mentions that permissible acts are written but then cancelled. Does that make you more mindful of your everyday actions, knowing they are noted in your book? How can you turn a permissible act into a good deed?
?
Consider that for the believer, sins are forgiven and erased, while for the disbeliever, good deeds are rejected. What does this teach you about the importance of faith beyond just actions? How does this affect your hope in Allah's mercy and your fear of His justice?

Writing Forgetful Acts Without Accountability
Explains that forgetful actions are written but not punishable, as writing serves documentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even actions done forgetfully are recorded by angels, but the person is not punished because the purpose of writing is documentation, not retribution.

Core Idea
Forgetful acts are written, but not punished.

The second line commented on by the author is 'walaw dhalil' (even if forgetful). Here Sanusi explains that an action performed while forgetful or heedless is still recorded by the angels. However, he emphasizes that the purpose of writing is not to punish but to document. This lesson clarifies the distinction between recording and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on forgetfulness
Focus on the section starting with 'wa law dhalil.' Read it in Arabic and translation.
2
Understand the definition of dhuhul
Note that dhuhul means heedlessness or forgetfulness. The action is still written.
3
Grasp the purpose of writing
The author explicitly says the purpose is not punishment or reward. Think about what that implies for divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئاً فَعَلْ أي: لن يتركوا من شأنه وحاله شيئاً فعله بلا كتابة بل يكتبونه قولاً أو غيره، فليست الكتابة مختصة بالأقوال، وإن كان قوله تعالى: ﴿مَا يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَيدٌ﴾ [ق: ۱۸] في خصوص الأقوال، وكذا حديث ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما في تفسير الآية المذكورة ) ، فإنه قال : يكتب كل ما يتكلم به من خير أو شر حتى إنه ليكتب قوله : أكلت شربت، ذهبت، جنت، رأيت، حتى إذا كان الخميس ويوم الاثنين عُرض قوله وعمله، فأقرَّ منه ما كان يوم خيراً أو شرًا وألغي سائره؛ أي باقيه وهو المباح والمكروه، فتلتقمه حيتان البحر فتموت منه لنتنه ، فتخرج منه دود يأكل الزرع، وهذا صريح في كتب المباحات، فيؤيد القول بكتابتها، لكن تقدَّم أن بعضهم اعتمد عدم كتابتها، وظواهر الآثار أن الحسنات تكتب مميزة عن السيئات فقيل : إن سيئات المؤمن أول كتابه، وآخره هذه الذنوب قد سترتها ،وغفرتها، وحسنات الكافر أول كتابه وآخره هذه حسناتك قد رددتها عليك وما قبلتها . قوله : ( ولو ذَهِلٌ) أي : ولو غَفَل ونسي ؛ فالذهول عن الشيء نسيانه والغفلة عنه، فيكتب ما فعله نسياناً، وإن كان لا يؤاخذ به ؛ لأنَّه ليس الغرض من الكتابة المعاقبة، ولا الإثابة.
His statement: 'They will not neglect from his affair anything he did' means: they will not leave out of his state and condition anything he did without writing; rather, they write it, whether speech or otherwise. So writing is not exclusive to words, even though Allah's statement: 'Not a word does he utter but there is with him a watcher, ready' (Q. 50:18) is specific to words. Likewise, the hadith of Ibn Abbas in the interpretation of the mentioned verse: He said: He writes everything that he speaks of good or evil, until he writes his saying: I ate, I drank, I went, I came, I saw. Then on Thursday and Monday, his speech and deed are presented, and what was good or evil is confirmed, and the remainder is cancelled; i.e., the rest of it, which is the permissible (mubah) and the disliked (makruh). Then fish of the sea swallow it and die from its foulness, then worms come out of it that eat the crops. And this is explicit about the writing of permissible acts, so it supports the opinion of writing them. But it has preceded that some relied on not writing them. And the apparent meanings of the traditions are that good deeds are written distinct from evil deeds, so it is said: Indeed, the sins of the believer are the first of his book, and its end: 'These sins I have veiled and forgiven.' And the good deeds of the disbeliever are the first of his book and its end: 'These good deeds I have returned to you and not accepted them.' His statement: 'And even if forgetful' means: even if he is heedless and forgetful; for heedlessness of something is its forgetfulness and neglect. So he writes what he did forgetfully, even if he is not punished for it, because the purpose of writing is not punishment nor reward.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on two lines from the poem: 'They will not neglect from his affair anything he did' and 'And even if forgetful.' The author explains the first line by clarifying that the angels write everything a person does, not just speech, even though the verse in Surat Qaf specifically mentions words. He then cites a hadith from Ibn Abbas that expands on this: the angels write every utterance, even mundane things like 'I ate, I drank,' etc. Then, on Mondays and Thursdays, deeds are presented; good and evil deeds are confirmed, but permissible (mubah) and disliked (makruh) acts are cancelled and given to fish that die from their foulness. This supports the view that permissible acts are written, though some scholars hold they are not written. The author then notes that traditions indicate good and evil deeds are recorded distinctly, and he mentions an interpretation: for the believer, sins are written first but then forgiven; for the disbeliever, good deeds are written first but then rejected. Finally, he explains the second line: 'even if forgetful' means that even if one acts forgetfully, the angel writes it, but the person is not held accountable because the purpose of writing is not punishment or reward but documentation.

Key Terms
ذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgetful actions are not recorded.
Correction: The passage says they are recorded but not punished.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is a person not punished for a forgetful action?
A) Because the angel does not write it
B) Because the purpose of writing is not punishment
C) Because forgetfulness is excused completely
D) Because only deliberate sins are written

Key Takeaway
Angels document all actions, even forgetful ones, but accountability is separate; forgetfulness removes punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If forgetful acts are written but not punished, what does this teach you about God's justice and mercy? How can you cultivate greater consciousness (taqwa) to reduce forgetfulness in obedience?

Locations of the Two Recording Angels
The commentator enumerates opinions on the location of the two recording angels and recommends refraining from settling on a specific one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on the location of the two recording angels and the safest approach to this matter.

Core Idea
The recording angels are not confined to a single spot; their location varies with the person's state.

The location of the two recording angels on the human body is a subject of difference among scholars. This passage presents multiple reports and a key principle: it is safest to refrain from settling on a fixed location. By studying these opinions, we learn the proper attitude towards non-essential details of the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

Notice the variety of body parts mentioned.
2

These represent the differing views.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Note how the commentator reconciles the differing views: the angels are not confined to a single place.
This is a key principle.
4
Learn the safe approach
The commentator advises that the safest course is to refrain from settling on a specific location.
This teaches humility before the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في محلّهما من الشخص، فقيل : ناجذاه؛ أي : آخر أضراسه الأيمن والأيسر، وقيل عاتقاه، وقيل ذقنه، وقيل شفتاه، وقيل عنفقته، ورُوي عن مجاهد أنه إن قعد كان أحدهما عن يمينه والآخر عن يساره، وإن مشى كان أحدهما أمامه والآخر وراءه، وإن رقد كان أحدهما عند رأسه والآخر عند رجليه، ويجمع بين هذه الأقاويل بأنهما لا يلزمان محلاً واحداً، والأسلم في أمثال ذلك الوقف.
There is disagreement regarding their location on the person. It is said: his two molars, i.e., his farthest molars on the right and left. It is also said: his two shoulders, his chin, his lips, his beard. And it is narrated from Mujahid that if he sits, one is on his right and the other on his left; if he walks, one is in front and the other behind; if he lies down, one is at his head and the other at his feet. These sayings are reconciled by the fact that they are not confined to a single place. The safest course regarding such matters is to refrain from settling on a specific location.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the different opinions regarding the physical location of the two recording angels (al-malakan al-hafizan) on the human body. The author presents various reports: some say on the molars, some on the shoulders, some on the chin, lips, or beard. He then cites a narration from Mujahid that their position changes with the person's state. The author concludes by stating that it is safest to refrain from settling on one location, as the angels are not confined to a single spot.

Key Terms

الملكان الكاتبان

Correction: The scholars present varying opinions, and Mujahid indicates the location changes with the person's state. The commentator reconciles by saying they are not confined to one place, so no single fixed location is correct.

Recording of Permissible Acts
Commentator argues for recording of permissible acts using Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the recording angels write down every word and deed, including permissible actions, and how this is supported by Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
Angels record every action, even the mundane, as nothing escapes their pen.

This passage addresses a common question: are only sinful or good deeds recorded? The commentator explains that based on the Quran and a hadith, even permissible actions like eating and drinking are written down. This shows the comprehensive nature of divine record-keeping.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the commentator builds his argument from the Quranic verse and the hadith.
The passage includes a vivid description of what happens to permissible actions after presentation.
2
Identify the key evidence
Find the Quranic verse (Qaf 50:18) and the hadith of Ibn Abbas. Understand how each supports the recording of permissible acts.
The hadith explicitly mentions recording even trivial statements like 'I ate' or 'I drank'.
3
Understand the differing opinion
The commentator acknowledges that some scholars held permissible acts are not recorded. See how he uses the hadith to lean toward the view that they are recorded.
The phrase 'but it was previously mentioned that some relied on not recording them' shows awareness of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَنْ يُهْمِلُوا مِنْ أَمْرِهِ شَيْئاً فَعَلْ) أي: لن يتركوا من شأنه وحاله شيئاً فعله بلا كتابة بل يكتبونه قولاً أو غيره، فليست الكتابة مختصة بالأقوال، وإن كان قوله تعالى: ﴿مَا يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَتِيدٌ﴾ [ق: ۱۸] في خصوص الأقوال، وكذا حديث ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما في تفسير الآية المذكورة، فإنه قال : يكتب كل ما يتكلم به من خير أو شر حتى إنه ليكتب قوله : أكلت شربت، ذهبت، جنت، رأيت، حتى إذا كان الخميس ويوم الاثنين عُرض قوله وعمله، فأقرَّ منه ما كان يوم خيراً أو شرًا وألغي سائره؛ أي باقيه وهو المباح والمكروه، فتلتقمه حيتان البحر فتموت منه لنتنه، فتخرج منه دود يأكل الزرع، وهذا صريح في كتب المباحات، فيؤيد القول بكتابتها، لكن تقدَّم أن بعضهم اعتمد عدم كتابتها، وظواهر الآثار أن الحسنات تكتب مميزة عن السيئات فقيل : إن سيئات المؤمن أول كتابه، وآخره هذه الذنوب قد سترتها، وغفرتها، وحسنات الكافر أول كتابه وآخره هذه حسناتك قد رددتها عليك وما قبلتها.
His statement: 'They will not neglect anything of his affair that he has done,' meaning: They will not leave any of his circumstances and states that he has done without recording; rather, they write it down, whether it is speech or otherwise. Thus, writing is not specific to speech, even though Allah's statement 'He does not utter a word except that he has a ready watcher' (Qaf:18) is specific to speech. Likewise, the hadith of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) in the interpretation of the aforementioned verse he said: He writes everything that one speaks, whether good or evil, until he writes his saying: I ate, I drank, I went, I came, I saw. Then on Thursday and Monday, his speech and actions are presented. Then whatever was good or evil on that day is confirmed, and the rest is nullified, i.e., the remainder which is the permissible and the disliked. Then the fish of the sea swallow it, and they die from its stench, and worms come out of it that eat the crops. This is explicit about the recording of permissible acts. So it supports the opinion of recording them, but it was previously mentioned that some relied on not recording them. The apparent meanings of the traditions indicate that good deeds are written distinctly from evil deeds. So it is said: The evil deeds of the believer are at the beginning of his book, and at the end it is said: These sins I have concealed and forgiven. And the good deeds of the disbeliever are at the beginning of his book, and at the end: These good deeds I have returned to you and not accepted.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the two recording angels (al-malakan al-katiban) write down everything a person does or says, including permissible (mubah) actions. The author cites the Quranic verse (Qaf:18) and a hadith of Ibn Abbas which narrates that even statements like 'I ate, I drank' are recorded. The author acknowledges that some scholars held that permissible actions are not recorded, but he leans toward the view that they are, based on the general wording of the verse and the explicit hadith about permissible acts.

المباح
الملائكة الحفظة
ما يلفظ من قول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think only sinful or good speech is recorded by the angels.
Correction: The hadith of Ibn Abbas shows that even permissible statements like 'I ate' or 'I drank' are recorded, indicating that all speech is documented.

Checkpoint

A) Permissible actions are not recorded.
B) Permissible actions are recorded.
C) Only evil actions are recorded.
D) Only good actions are recorded.

Key Takeaway
The recording angels write down everything, including permissible acts, as confirmed by the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Recording of Forgetful Actions
The commentator clarifies that forgetful actions are recorded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that actions performed in forgetfulness are still recorded by the angels, though not necessarily for punishment or reward.

Core Idea
Forgetful actions are still recorded, but not for punishment.

This passage addresses a common theological question: are actions done unintentionally written down? The commentator clarifies that the recording angels note all actions, even those from forgetfulness, but the purpose of recording is not solely reward or punishment. Let's read the source.

These terms are closely related but have slight nuances.

Recording the Moan of the Sick Person
The commentator explains the recording of moans and advises saying 'Ah' as a name of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the angels record even involuntary sounds like a sick person's moan, and the importance of saying 'Ah' rather than 'Akh'.

Core Idea
Even your moans are recorded — so choose your words wisely.

This lesson continues the discussion of the Samiyyat (audible matters) related to angels. The commentator emphasizes the extent of angelic recording by noting that even the moan of a sick person is written down. He also provides a practical reminder about what a sick person should utter, connecting it to the names of Allah and Satan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the key terms like 'anin' and the names of Allah and Satan.
The passage is short but packed with legal and spiritual implications.
2
Understand the significance of recording moans
The commentator explains that even involuntary sounds are recorded. This shows the extent of Allah's knowledge and the angels' meticulous recording.
Contrast this with the idea that only deliberate deeds are accounted for.
3
Note the distinction between 'Ah' and 'Akh'
The sick person is advised to say 'Ah' because it is a name of Allah, and to avoid 'Akh' because it is a name of Satan. This is a practical application of the belief in the recording of all utterances.
4
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
The passage cites Quran 50:18 as proof that every word is recorded. Understand how this ayah supports the general rule.
The verse is used to show the generality of recording, including moans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (حَتَّى الأنيْنَ فِى الْمَرَض كَمَا نُقِلْ) أي حتى يكتبون الأنين الصادر منه في المرض، والأنين مصدر أنَّ الرجل يَئِنُّ إذا صَوَّت، وينبغي للمريض أن يقول : (آه)؛ لأنه ورد أنه من أسمائه تعالى ولا يقول (آخ) ; لأنه اسم من أسماء الشيطان. وقوله : (كما نُقِل) أي كما نقله أئمة الدين وعلماء المسلمين ومن أعظمهم الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه فإنه قال : يكتبون على العبد كل شيء حتى أنينه في مرضه، وتمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ما يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَتِيدٌ [ق: ۱۸]؛ لأن وقوع (قول) في سياق النفي يقتضي العموم.
His statement: 'Even the moan in illness, as it has been narrated,' meaning: even they write down the moan that comes from him during sickness. 'Anin' is the verbal noun of 'anna' meaning the man moans when he emits a sound. It is desirable for the sick person to say 'Ah' because it is reported that it is one of His names, may He be exalted, and he should not say 'Akh' because it is a name of Satan. His statement: 'as it has been narrated' means as the imams of the religion and scholars of the Muslims have narrated, the greatest of them being Imam Malik (may Allah be pleased with him). He said: They write down everything against the servant, even his moan during his sickness. They adduced the saying of Allah: 'He does not utter a word except that he has a ready watcher' (Qaf:18) because the occurrence of 'word' in a context of negation necessitates generality.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the comprehensiveness of the angelic recording. The author notes that even the moan (anin) of a sick person is recorded, citing Imam Malik. He also gives a practical admonition: the sick person should say 'Ah' rather than 'Akh' because the former is a name of Allah and the latter is a name of Satan.

الأنين
أسماء الله
الشيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage shows that even involuntary moans are recorded, based on the generality of the Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
Angels record every utterance, including a sick person's moan, so believers should be mindful and say what is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Recording of Even Involuntary Sounds
Commentator explains recording of even groans, citing Quran and transmission from Imam Malik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even involuntary sounds like groaning during sickness are recorded by the angels, as established by Quranic evidence and transmitted from early imams.

Core Idea
Every utterance, even a groan, is recorded — no speech escapes divine surveillance.

This passage from the commentary on the Jawharah al-Tawhid addresses an often-overlooked aspect of divine recording: it covers not only intentional speech but even involuntary sounds like groans of sickness. The commentator explains the linguistic meaning of 'anin', cites a practical sunna about what to say when in pain, and grounds the teaching in the Quranic verse about the vigilant recorder. By the end, you will see that nothing escapes Allah's recording.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope of recording
Read the passage to grasp that the angels write down everything, including the groaning that emanates from a sick person. Note the key term 'anin' (groaning).
This expands the common understanding that only deliberate speech is recorded.
2
Learn the linguistic and practical rulings
Identify the recommendation to say 'Āh' instead of 'Ākh' when in pain, and the reason: 'Āh' is reported to be a name of Allah, while 'Ākh' is a name of Satan.
This shows how the teaching applies to daily life.
3
See the Quranic proof
Focus on the verse 50:18 and the commentator's explanation that the word 'qawl' (word) in a negation context implies generality, covering even sounds like groaning.
The principle of 'umum (generality) is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات
وقوله : (حَتَّى الأنيْنَ فِى المَرَض كَمَا نُقِلْ أي حتى يكتبون الأنين الصادر منه في المرض، والأنين مصدر أنَّ الرجل يَئِنُّ إذا صَوَّت، وينبغي للمريض أن يقول : (آه)؛ لأنه ورد أنه من أسمائه تعالى ولا يقول (آخ) ؛ لأنه اسم من أسماء الشيطان. وقوله : (كما نُقِل) أي كما نقله أئمة الدين وعلماء المسلمين ومن أعظمهم الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه فإنه قال : يكتبون على العبد كل شيء حتى أنينه في مرضه، وتمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ما يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَتِيدٌ [ق: ۱۸]؛ لأن وقوع (قول) في سياق النفي يقتضي العموم.
The Sam'iyyat (matters of revelation). And His saying: 'Even the groaning in sickness as was transmitted' means: they write the groaning that emanates from him in sickness. And al-anin is the source of anna al-rajulu ya'innu if he makes a sound. And it is befitting for the sick person to say 'Ah' because it has been reported that it is one of His names, may He be exalted, and he should not say 'Akh' because it is a name from the names of Satan. And His saying: 'as was transmitted' means as the imams of religion and scholars of the Muslims transmitted, and among the greatest of them is Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, for he said: 'They write against the servant everything, even his groaning in his sickness.' And they adhered to His saying, exalted: 'He does not utter a single word except that there is an observer, ready [Qaf 50:18]' because the occurrence of 'word' in the context of negation necessitates generality.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of what is recorded by the angels. The commentator explains the line from the poem 'حَتَّى الأنيْنَ فِى المَرَض كَمَا نُقِلْ' meaning the recording of even groans in sickness. He clarifies that 'anin' is the sound of groaning. He then gives a practical ruling: the sick should say 'آه' (Ah) because it is reported to be a name of Allah, and avoid 'آخ' (Akh) because it is a name of Satan. Then he explains the source of this ruling: the reports transmitted by imams like Imam Malik that everything including groans is recorded. They also rely on the Quranic verse 50:18, which states that no word is uttered without a watcher. The commentator notes that the word 'قول' (word) appears in a negation context, which implies generality (عموم) meaning every utterance, including groans, is recorded. Thus the passage establishes that all speech, even involuntary sounds, are subject to divine recording.

The Obligation of Belief in Death
Commentator affirms obligation to believe in death and refutes materialists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why belief in death is an obligatory Islamic doctrine and how it refutes the materialist denial of an afterlife.

Core Idea
Death is not just a natural end—it is a matter of faith and a refutation of materialism.

The commentator begins by affirming that belief in death is a wajib (obligatory) aspect of faith. He then clarifies that this belief includes the general annihilation of all creatures, directly opposing the materialist (al-Dahriyya) worldview, which sees death as a mere biological cycle without a Creator. This short but pointed passage sets the doctrinal boundary between Islamic eschatology and atheistic naturalism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical structure
Notice that the sentence inverts the predicate and subject: 'obligatory is our belief in death.' This grammatical emphasis highlights the obligatory nature of the belief.
The Arabic is: 'وَوَاجِبُ إِيْمَانُنا بِالمَوتِ'.
2
Grasp the core obligation
The meaning is that our affirmation (tasdiq) of death is obligatory. This includes affirming the universal annihilation of all things (fana al-kull). Death is not just an event but a divinely decreed end for every creature.
The text says 'فَيَجِبُ التَّصْدِيقُ بِعُمُومِ فَنَاءِ الْكُلِّ'.
3
Recognize the opponent: al-Dahriyya
The passage explicitly refutes the Materialists (al-Dahriyya), who claim that life is only wombs that push out and earth that swallows—a purely natural cycle without a Creator or afterlife.
Their saying: 'إِنْ هِيَ إِلَّا أَرْحَامٌ تَدْفَعُ وَأَرْضٌ تَبْلَعُ'.
4
See the refutation
By making belief in death obligatory and universal, the commentator rejects the materialist reduction. Death is not an end in itself but leads to accountability and the afterlife. Our faith requires us to believe in the all-encompassing nature of death as a creation of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب الإيمان بالموتِ
۸۸ - وَوَاجِبُ إِيْمَانُنا بِالمَوتِ ويَقْبِضُ الرُّوحَ رَسُولُ المَوتِ قوله : (وَوَاجِبْ إِيْمَانُنا بِالمَوتِ) ، (واجب) خبرٌ مقدم، و(إيماننا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، و (بالموت) متعلق بإيماننا ؛ والمعنى: أن تصديقنا بالموت واجب، فيجب التصديق بعموم فناء الكل خلافاً للدَّهْرِيَّة ) في قولهم : إِنْ هي إلا أرحام تدفعُ وأرض تبلع .
The Obligation of Belief in Death. 88 - And obligatory is our belief in death, and the angel of death takes the soul. His saying: (And obligatory is our belief in death): (Obligatory) is a predicate placed forward, and (our belief) is a subject placed later, and (in death) is related to our belief. The meaning: our affirmation of death is obligatory, so it is obligatory to affirm the general annihilation of everything, contrary to the Materialists (al-Dahriyya) in their saying: 'It is only wombs that push out and earth that swallows.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage defines the doctrinal obligation to believe in death. The commentator explains the grammatical structure of the verse: 'obligatory' is the predicate, 'our belief' is the subject, and 'in death' is related to belief. The meaning is that we must affirm that death is universal and inevitable. This contradicts the materialist sect (al-Dahriyya) who claim that life is only natural cycles of birth and death without a Creator or afterlife. Their saying is paraphrased: 'It is only wombs that push out (babies) and earth that swallows (bodies).' The commentator affirms the opposite: death is decreed by Allah and all creatures will perish.

Correction: The commentator asserts that belief in death is obligatory and part of faith, and that death leads to the general annihilation of all creatures, which opposes the materialist denial of a Creator and afterlife.

Key Takeaway

The Obligatory Belief in Death and Refutation of Deniers
Author affirms obligatory belief in death and refutes two deviant views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in death is obligatory, includes the annihilation of all things, occurs by divine decree, and is rationally undeniable, contra the Dahriyya and philosophers.

Core Idea
Death is both rationally undeniable and divinely decreed—a truth rejected by materialists and misdefined by philosophers.

This passage establishes the Islamic doctrine of death as a matter of obligatory belief. The author explains that while the fact of death is self-evident to reason, its nature and purpose are defined by revelation. He then refutes two deviant views: the Dahriyya who deny any afterlife, and the philosophers who reduce death to a natural event. This lesson will guide you through the doctrinal affirmation and the refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once through, then read the translation to grasp the author's full argument.
Pay attention to the grammatical inversion in the opening line.
2
Understand the grammatical construction
The author parses the line: 'Wajib' is predicate, 'imanuna' is subject, 'bil-mawt' attached to 'imanuna'. This rhetorical emphasis underscores obligation.
3
Identify the two refutations
Note the two groups refuted: the Dahriyya (deny any afterlife) and the philosophers (view death as natural). Understand their errors.
The refutation of the Dahriyya is about the denial of annihilation; the refutation of philosophers is about denying the divine manner.
4
Recognize the rational basis for belief
The author asserts that the occurrence of death is witnessed, so no scriptural proof is needed for its reality—only its manner requires revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَوَاجِبْ إِيْمَانُنا بِالمَوتِ) ، (واجب) خبرٌ مقدم، و(إيماننا) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، و (بالموت) متعلق بإيماننا ؛ والمعنى: أن تصديقنا بالموت واجب، فيجب التصديق بعموم فناء الكل خلافاً للدَّهْرِيَّة في قولهم : إن هي إلا أرحام تدفعُ وأرض تبلع . ويجب التصديق أيضاً بأنه على الوجه المعهود شرعاً من فراغ الآجال المقدرة خلافاً للحكماء في قولهم بأنه بمجرد اختلال نظام الطبيعة، فمراد المصنف بذلك الرد على من ذكر . وأما أصلُ وقوع الموت فلا حاجة للنص عليه؛ لأنه لا يشك فيه عاقل لكونه مشاهداً، ويدل على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّكَ مَيِّتٌ وَإِنَّهُم مَّيِّتُونَ﴾ [الزمر: ٣٠]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٨٥]، والأحاديث فيه كثيرة.
And his saying: (And obligatory is our belief in death). (Wajib) is a predicate forward, and (imanuna) is a subject delayed, and (bil-mawt) is related to our belief. The meaning: that our affirmation of death is obligatory. So it is obligatory to affirm the annihilation of all things, contrary to the Dahriyya who say: 'It is only wombs that push and earth that swallows.' And it is also obligatory to affirm that death occurs in the manner legislatively known, when predestined terms are completed, contrary to the philosophers who say that it is merely due to the disruption of the natural system. So the author's intent by that is to refute those mentioned. As for the actual occurrence of death, there is no need for scriptural evidence because no rational person doubts it due to its being witnessed. And that is evidenced by His saying: 'Indeed, you are to die, and indeed, they are to die' (Quran 39:30), and His saying: 'Every soul will taste death' (Quran 3:185), and the hadiths on it are many.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The author explains the line 'wa wajibu imanuna bil-mawt' (and obligatory is our belief in death). He breaks down the grammar: 'wajib' is the predicate, 'imanuna' is the subject, 'bil-mawt' is related to 'imanuna'. The meaning: it is obligatory to affirm death. Then he refutes two groups: the Dahriyya (materialists) who deny any afterlife and say death is just biological, and the philosophers (hukama) who say death is merely a natural disruption. The author asserts that the occurrence of death is known by observation and does not need scriptural proof, but scripture confirms it. He cites Quran 39:30 and 3:185.

Misconception: Belief in death requires scriptural evidence; without it, one could doubt it.
Correction: The author states that death's occurrence is witnessed by all rational people, so it is rationally known without needing scripture, though scripture confirms it.

Belief in death is obligatory and includes both its occurrence (known by reason) and its Sharia-determined manner (known by revelation).

The Nature of Death: Existential or Non-Existential?
Dispute on whether death is existential or non-existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and non-Ash'ari views on whether death is an existential attribute or the absence of life, and the evidence and approach to such theological disputes.

Core Idea
Death: a created attribute or just the absence of life? The debate reveals how kalam interprets Quran and hadith.

In this lesson, we examine a classical dispute in kalam: is death a real, existential attribute that opposes life, or is it merely the privation of life? The passage presents the Ash'ari position and its opponents, along with scriptural evidence and a final recommendation to entrust the matter to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses technical terms like wujudi, adami, kayfiyya, taddad, and 'adam wa milka.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find where al-Ash'ari's view and the view of al-Isfarayini and al-Zamakhshari are stated. Note how each defines death and characterizes the opposition between life and death.
Al-Ash'ari sees death as a quality opposing life; the others see it as absence of life.
3
Understand the types of opposition
The passage mentions two types: taqabul al-tadadd (contradiction) and taqabul al-'adam wa al-milka (privation and possession). Understand how each applies to death.
These are logical categories in kalam.
4
Analyze the scriptural evidence
Examine the Quranic verse (67:2) and the hadith about death as a ram. See how al-Ash'ari uses them and how opponents reinterpret 'creation' as 'decree'.
The author notes that the opponents' interpretation contradicts the apparent meaning.
5
Reflect on the conclusion: tafwid
The author ends by recommending tafwid, entrusting the true knowledge to Allah. Consider why this is the best approach in such matters.
This shows humility and caution in theological speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف في الموت هل هو وجودي أو عدمي؟ فذهب الأشعري رحمه الله تعالى إلى الأول، وعرَّفه بأنه كيفية ؛ أي: صفة وجودية تضاد الحياة فالتقابل بينهما تقابل التضاد. وذهب الإسفراينيُّ والزمخشري إلى الثاني، وعرَّفاه بأنه عدم الحياة عمَّا مِنْ شأنه أن يكون حيًّا ، فالتقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة. ويدلُّ للأول قوله تعالى : الَّذِى خَلَقَ المَوْتَ وَالحَيَاةَ ﴾ [الملك: ٢]، وتأويل الخلق بالتقدير، كما قاله مَنْ ذهب إلى أنه عدمي خلاف الظاهر، وفي بعض الأحاديث: «إنَّ الله خلق الموتَ في صورة كبش لا يمرُّ بشيء إلا مات، كما أن في بعض الأحاديث: «إنَّ الحياة خلقها الله تعالى على صورة فرس لا تمرُّ بشيءٍ إلا حيي. وهذا إنما هو باعتبار التمثيل، وإلا فالموتُ صفة للميت، كما أن الحياة صفة للحي، والأولى التفويضُ فى أمثال هذه المقامات .
And there is disagreement concerning death: is it existential or non-existential? Al-Ash'ari (may Allah have mercy on him) went to the first, and defined it as a quality (kayfiyya), i.e., an existential attribute that opposes life, so the opposition between them is opposition of contradiction. Al-Isfarayini and al-Zamakhshari went to the second, and defined it as the absence of life from that which is by nature living, so the opposition between them is opposition of privation and possession. And evidence for the first is His saying: 'He who created death and life' (Quran 67:2). And interpreting 'creation' as 'decree' (taqdir), as said by those who hold it is non-existential, is contrary to the apparent meaning. And in some hadiths: 'Indeed, Allah created death in the form of a ram that passes by nothing but it dies,' and also in some hadiths: 'Indeed, Allah created life in the form of a horse that passes by nothing but it lives.' And this is only by way of representation. Otherwise, death is an attribute of the dead, just as life is an attribute of the living. And the best course is to entrust (tafwid) knowledge in such matters.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a theological classification and argument. The author presents the ikhtilaf (disagreement) about the nature of death: whether it is an existential attribute (wujudi) or a non-existential attribute (adami). Al-Ash'ari (who founded the Ash'ari school) held that death is an existential quality (kayfiyya) that opposes life, making them opposites in the sense of contradiction (taqabul al-tadadd). Al-Isfarayini and al-Zamakhshari (scholars from other schools) held that death is simply the absence of life from something that could have life, making the opposition one of privation and possession (taqabul al-adam wa al-milka). The author then gives evidence for the Ash'ari view: Quran 67:2 'He who created death and life' – the verb 'created' suggests death is a created thing, so it is existential. Those who say it is non-existential interpret 'creation' as 'decree' (taqdir), but the author says this is contrary to the apparent meaning (khilaf al-zahir). He also cites hadiths where death is personified as a ram and life as a horse, but these are metaphorical (tamthil). He concludes that the best approach is tafwid (entrusting the true knowledge to Allah) in such matters, because the reality is beyond our comprehension.

Key Terms
موت
وجودي
عدمي
كيفية
تضاد
عدم وملكة
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this disagreement implies uncertainty in Islamic creed, whereas it is a secondary issue of interpretation.
Correction: The dispute is a matter of theological interpretation, not a foundational creedal difference. The author recommends tafwid, showing that certainty is not required on such details.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence al-Ash'ari uses to support that death is existential?
A) A hadith
B) Reason
C) Quran 67:2
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
The debate on death's nature shows how kalam balances scriptural evidence with rational categories, ultimately recommending tafwid when certainty is elusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith about death being created as a ram, do you tend to picture it literally or metaphorically? How does your instinct reveal your underlying assumption about whether unseen realities are 'things' or mere absences?
?
The author advises tafwid (entrusting) in such matters. Is there a theological question you have been trying to solve with your own reasoning that you might need to entrust to Allah? What would it cost you to release that need for certainty?
?
If death is an existential attribute created by Allah, then it is a creature you will one day encounter. How does that change your attitude toward preparing for it, compared to thinking of death as simply not being alive?

The Angel of Death: Izra'il and His Magnificent Description
Explanatory description of the angel of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity and description of the angel of death, Izra'il, as explained by the author.

Core Idea
The angel of death is a colossal being with helpers, appointed by Allah.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a line from the poem that mentions the messenger of death. He identifies this messenger as the angel Izra'il, explains the meaning of his name, and provides a vivid description of his immense size, his relationship to the Preserved Tablet, and the helpers he commands. This lesson will help you visualize the angelic being entrusted with taking souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice the key phrases and terms used to describe the angel.
Pay attention to the opening line from the poem.
2
Understand the name 'Izra'il'
The author states that 'Izra'il' means 'servant of the Almighty' (Abd al-Jabbar). Reflect on the significance of this name as a description of his role and nature.
3
Visualize the physical description
The passage describes the angel's head in the highest heaven, feet in the lowest earth, face opposite the Preserved Tablet, and all creation between his eyes. Try to form a mental image of this majestic being.
4
Note the helpers and their role
The author mentions that Izra'il has helpers equal to the number of those who die. Understand that he does not personally take every soul; rather, he has assistants.
This corrects the misconception that he acts alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويَقْبِضُ الرُّوحَ رَسُولُ المَوتِ) أي: يخرجها من مقرها الملك الموكل بالموت؛ وهو عزرائيل عليه السلام ومعناه عبد الجبار وهو ملك عظيم هائل المنظر مُفزع جدًّا، رأسه في السماء العليا، ورجلاه في تخوم الأرض السفلى؛ أي: منتهاها، ووجهه مقابل اللوح المحفوظ، والخلق بين عينيه وله أعوان بعدد من يموت، يترفق
And his saying: (And the messenger of death seizes the soul) meaning: the angel entrusted with death extracts it from its place; and he is 'Izra'il (peace be upon him), and his meaning is 'servant of the Almighty' (Abd al-Jabbar), and he is a great angel, terrifying in appearance, very frightening. His head is in the highest heaven, and his feet are in the depths of the lowest earth; i.e., its utmost limit. His face is opposite the Preserved Tablet, and all creation is between his eyes. He has helpers equal to the number of those who die, and he treats gently...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a line from the poem: 'wa yaqbīḍu al-rūḥa rasūlu al-mawt' (and the messenger of death seizes the soul). The author clarifies that the 'messenger of death' is the angel entrusted with death, named 'Izra'il, which means 'servant of the Almighty' ('Abd al-Jabbar). He then describes this angel's magnitude: his head is in the highest heaven, his feet in the lowest earth, his face opposite the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-mahfūẓ), and all creation is between his eyes. He has numerous helpers, one for each person who dies. The text ends mid-sentence with 'yataraffaq' (he treats gently), indicating he shows mercy in taking souls.

Correction: The passage states that Izra'il has helpers equal to the number of those who die, implying he delegates the task.

The Angel of Death's Appearance
Narration of Ibrahim's request to see the Angel of Death's forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith describing the Angel of Death's two forms for believers and disbelievers and its theological significance.

Core Idea
The Angel of Death appears terrifying to disbelievers and beautiful to believers.

The commentator narrates a powerful hadith in which Prophet Ibrahim asks the Angel of Death to show him how he takes souls. This hadith illustrates the profound difference in the death experience based on one's faith. As you read, notice the vivid imagery and the moral lesson it conveys.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith narrative
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the story's vivid descriptions sink in.
Notice the contrast between the two forms.
2
Compare the two forms
Identify the description given for the disbeliever's form and the believer's form. Note the details: color, size, fire, beauty, scent, clothing.
The disbeliever's form is a black man with fire; the believer's is a beautiful young man in white.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Ibrahim felt each form alone would suffice to cause fear or joy. Think about the spiritual meaning for the dying person.
This hadith emphasizes that death is not the same for everyone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمؤمن ويأتيه في صورة حسنة دون غيره، وفي حديث ابن مسعود وابن عباس : أن إبراهيم عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : يا ملك الموت؛ أرني كيف تقبض أنفاس الكفار ؟ قال : يا إبراهيمُ لا تطيق ذلك، قال: بلى، ثم قال : أعرض فأعرض ثم ، فإذا هو برجل أسود ينال رأسه السماء، يخرج من فيه لهب النار، فغشي على إبراهيم، ثم أفاق، وقد تحول ملك الموت في الصورة الأولى، فقال: يا ملك الموت؛ لو لم يلق الكافرُ من البلاء والحزن إلا صورتك هذه لكفاه، فأرني كيف تقبض أنفاس المؤمنين؟ قال: أعرض فأعرض ثم التفت، فإذا هو برجل شاب أحسن الناس وجهاً، وأطيبهم ريحاً في ثياب بيض ، فقال : يا ملك الموت؛ لو لم يرَ المؤمنُ عند موته من قرة العين والكرامة إلا صورتَكَ هذه لكان يكفيه . وفي النظم إفادة جوهرية الروح، وإلا لم تُقبض ومذهب أهل السنة من المتكلمين والمحدثين والفقهاء والصوفية : أنها جسم لطيف مشتبك بالبدن كاشتباك الماء بالعود الأخضر، وبهذا جزم النووي. ومذهب جماعة من الصوفية والمعتزلة أنها ليست بجسم ولا عَرَضٍ، بل جوهر سجرد متعلّق بالبدن للتدبير، غير داخل فيه، ولا خارج عنه
He comes to the believer in a beautiful form unlike others. In the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Abbas: that Ibrahim (peace be upon him) said: O Angel of Death; show me how you take the breaths of the disbelievers? He said: O Ibrahim, you cannot bear that. He said: Yes (I can). Then he said: Show (me), so he showed him, and behold, it was a black man whose head reached the sky, from whose mouth fire was coming out, so Ibrahim fainted. Then he recovered, and the Angel of Death had transformed to the first form. He said: O Angel of Death; if the disbeliever does not meet from affliction and sorrow except this form of yours, it would suffice him. So show me how you take the breaths of the believers? He said: Show, so he showed him, then he turned around, and behold, it was a young man, the most beautiful of people in face, and the most pleasant in scent, in white garments. He said: O Angel of Death; if the believer does not see at his death from delight and honor except this form of yours, it would suffice him. And in the poem (al-nazm) there is an indication that the soul is a substance (jawhariyya), otherwise it would not be taken. And the school of Ahl al-Sunna from the mutakallimun, muhaddithun, fuqaha', and sufis: that it is a subtle body (jism latif) intertwined with the body like the intertwining of water with green wood. An-Nawawi affirmed this. And the school of a group of sufis and the Mu'tazila: that it is not a body nor an accident ('arad), but a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) connected to the body for management, not inside it nor outside it.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage consists of two related sections. First, it narrates a hadith in which Ibrahim (peace be upon him) asks the Angel of Death to show him how he takes the souls of disbelievers and believers. The Angel appears to disbelievers in a terrifying black form with fire, and to believers in a beautiful young form, demonstrating that the experience of death differs according to one's state. Second, the commentator extracts from the poem the implication that the soul must be a substance (jawhar), because it is 'taken' (qubid) by the Angel. He then presents two theological views on the nature of the soul. The first, held by the majority of Ahl al-Sunna (including mutakallimun, muhaddithun, jurists and sufis), is that the soul is a subtle body (jism latif) intertwined with the physical body, like water in green wood. Al-Nawawi affirmed this. The second view, held by some sufis and the Mu'tazila, is that the soul is not a body nor an accident, but a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that is connected to the body for its management, without being inside or outside it. The commentator does not explicitly prefer one view, but presents both as legitimate positions.

Key Terms
ملك الموت
المؤمن
الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think death is a neutral, uniform experience for everyone.
Correction: This hadith shows that the Angel of Death appears differently based on one's spiritual state: terrifying for disbelievers and beautiful for believers.

Checkpoint

B) A young man in white with beautiful face and pleasant scent
C) An old man in rags
D) A lion

Key Takeaway
The Angel of Death's form reflects the spiritual state of the dying person.

?

?
Ibrahim, a prophet, was overwhelmed by the terrifying form. If you were shown that form today, would it change how you live? What specific sin or neglect of duty would you address immediately?
?
The believer sees delight at death. Ask yourself: if you were to die tonight, would your soul depart with joy or regret? What is one concrete step you can take today to ensure your death becomes a moment of honor?

Two Views on the Nature of the Soul
Enumeration of two theological schools on the soul's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theological views on the nature of the soul: as a subtle body (jism latif) or as a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad).

Core Idea
The soul is either a subtle body intertwined with the body or an immaterial substance.

After narrating the hadith, the commentator presents a theological discussion on the nature of the soul. The poem indicates that the soul must be a substance (jawhar) because it is 'taken' by the Angel. Then two schools are mentioned: the majority view of Ahl al-Sunna and a minority view from some sufis and the Mu'tazila. This passage is crucial for understanding Islamic views on the soul's ontology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the implication from the poem
The commentator says the poem's wording implies the soul is a substance (jawhariyya), otherwise it wouldn't be 'taken' (qubid). Reflect on why 'taking' implies substance.
Substance can be grasped; accidents cannot be 'taken'.
2
Identify the majority view of Ahl al-Sunna
Find the description of the soul as a subtle body (jism latif) intertwined with the body like water in green wood. Note that an-Nawawi affirmed this.
This view is held by mutakallimun, muhaddithun, fuqaha', and sufis.
3
Identify the minority view
Find the alternative view: the soul is not a body nor an accident, but a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) connected to the body for management, neither inside nor outside.
This view is held by some sufis and the Mu'tazila.

The Generality of 'Al-Ruh'
Commentator defines 'al' in 'al-ruh' as for total inclusion, listing souls included and refuting opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the definite article in 'al-ruh' indicates total inclusion, encompassing all souls, and how this refutes the views of the Mu'tazila and innovators.

Core Idea
Al' in 'al-ruh' means all souls—human, jinn, angels, and even animals—leaving no exception.

The commentator's explanation of the word 'al-ruh' in the poem addresses a key theological debate: which souls does the Angel of Death take? By analyzing the definite article, he establishes the comprehensive scope and refutes opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'al' for istighraq
Read the first part of the passage where the commentator explains that 'al' indicates total inclusion. Notice that it specifies all souls of the thaqalayn, angels, and animals.
The word 'istighraq' means total inclusion.
2
Identify the three groups of souls mentioned
List the souls explicitly included: those of the thaqalayn (humans and jinn), angels (even his own soul), and animals/birds/insects.
This shows the scope is universal.
3
Notice the refutation of Mu'tazila and innovators
The commentator quotes the views of the Mu'tazila (who exclude non-thaqalayn) and the innovators (who exclude animals). Then he points out that the word 'al' itself refutes both.
This is a key argument: the grammar alone settles the debate.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why the definite article is sufficient to refute. The 'al' for istighraq is a well-known grammatical principle, so the poet's use of it settles the issue conclusively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(أل): في الروح للاستغراق، فهي دالة على العموم، والمراد جميع أرواح الثقلين ولو أرواح الشهداء براً وبحراً، وأرواح الملائكة حتى روح نفسه على أحد القولين ، وقيل : القابض لروحه هو الله عز وجل وأرواح البهائم والطيور وغيرهم، ولو بعوضة كما ذهب إليه أهل الحق، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يقبض أرواح غير الثقلين من الملائكة والطيور وغيرهم، وللمبتدعة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يقبض أرواح البهائم، بل يقبضها أعوانه، وقد أشار المصنّف للرد على الجميع بأل الدالة على العموم، ولمباشرة ملك الموت لذلك أسند إليه التوفي، كما في قوله تعالى : وَقُلْ يَتَوَفَّنَكُم مَّلَكُ الْمَوْتِ الَّذِى وُكِّلَ بِكُمْ [السجدة: 11]، وكَنسبته إلى أعوانه لمعالجتهم نزعها من العصب والعظم والعروق في قوله تعالى: تَوَفَّتْهُ رُسُلُنَا [الأنعام: 61]، وأما إسناد التوفي إليه تعالى في قوله تعالى : اللَّهُ يَتَوَفَّى الْأَنفُسَ حِينَ مَوْتِهَا [الزمر: 42]؛ فلأنه الخالق لذلك حقيقة الموجد له.
فائدة مجيء الموت والعبد على عمل صالح يسهل الموت، وكذلك السواك فيما ذكره جماعةٌ، ومما يُسهل الموت وجميع ما بعده من الأهوال ما ذكره السنوسي وغيره من صلاة ركعتين ليلة الجمعة بعد المغرب يقرأ بعد الفاتحة الزلزلة خمس عشرة، ورُوي أن سورتها تعدل نصف القرآن.
And 'al' in 'al-ruh' is for total inclusion (istighraq), so it indicates generality, and what is meant is all the souls of the two heavy groups (thaqalayn: humans and jinn) even the souls of martyrs on land and sea, and the souls of angels, even his own soul according to one of the two sayings. And it is said: the one who takes his soul is Allah, may He be exalted. And the souls of animals, birds, and others, even a mosquito, as Ahl al-Haqq went to, contrary to the Mu'tazila who went to that he does not take the souls of other than the thaqalayn from the angels, birds, etc., and contrary to the innovators (mubtadi'a) who went to that he does not take the souls of animals, rather his helpers take them. And the author indicated refutation of all by 'al' which indicates generality. And because the Angel of Death directly does that, the taking (tawaffi) is attributed to him, as in His saying: 'And say: the Angel of Death who is entrusted with you will take you' (Sajda 11), and like attribution to his helpers for their handling of its extraction from nerves, bones, and veins in His saying: 'Our messengers take him' (An'am 61). And as for attribution of tawaffi to Him, the Exalted, in His saying: 'Allah takes the souls at the time of their death' (Zumar 42); because He is the Creator of that, the true originator (al-mujid).
Benefit: The coming of death upon the servant while he is in a righteous deed facilitates death, and likewise the siwak, as a group mentioned. And among that which facilitates death and all the terrors after it is what Sanusi and others mentioned: two rak'ahs on Friday night after maghrib, reciting after al-Fatiha [surah] al-Zalzala fifteen times, and it is narrated that its surah is equivalent to half the Quran.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage clarifies two connected issues: the scope of the word 'al-ruh' (the soul) in the poem, and the correct attribution of death. First, the commentator states that the definite article 'al' in 'al-ruh' is for 'istighraq' (total inclusion), meaning it encompasses all souls: humans, jinn, angels, martyrs, and even animals. He then refutes two opposing views: the Mu'tazila who deny that the Angel of Death takes souls of other than humans and jinn, and the innovators (mubtadi'a) who deny that he takes animal souls, claiming his helpers do. The poem's use of 'al' refutes both. Second, the passage explains the different attributions of death (tawaffi) in the Quran. It is attributed to the Angel of Death because he directly executes the taking; to his helpers (angels) because they physically extract the soul; and to Allah because He is the true Creator and Originator of the act. This resolves apparent contradictions in Quranic verses. Finally, a 'benefit' (fa'ida) is added: performing righteous deeds, using a siwak, and a specific prayer on Friday night can ease the pangs of death and the terrors that follow.

Key Terms
الاستغراق
الثقلين
المبتدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Angel of Death only takes human souls.
Correction: The passage states that 'al' for istighraq includes all souls: humans, jinn, angels, and animals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the definite article 'al' in 'al-ruh' indicate?
A) It specifies a particular group of souls.
B) It indicates total inclusion of all souls.
C) It limits the meaning to humans only.
D) It is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The definite article 'al' in 'al-ruh' indicates that the Angel of Death takes all souls without exception, refuting those who narrow its scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Angel of Death takes every soul, even the mosquito's, what does that imply about the comprehensiveness of Allah's dominion? Is there any area of your life where you unconsciously believe that Allah does not have direct control?
?
The Mu'tazila and innovators limited the Angel's role based on their own reasoning. In your heart, have you ever placed limits on Allah's power or mercy, based on your own understanding rather than revelation? What would it mean to surrender that limitation today?
?
The Prophet's own soul is included in the generality according to one view. How does knowing that even the most beloved creation submits to death by Allah's decree reshape your attitude toward your own mortality? Are you living with the urgency that such a reality demands?

Attribution of Death and Its Easing
Commentator explains the attributions of death in the Quran and adds a benefit about easing death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran attributes death to the Angel of Death, his helpers, and Allah without contradiction, and learn a practice that eases death.

Core Idea
Death is attributed to Allah as Creator, to the Angel of Death as agent, and to helpers as physically extracting the soul.

The passage resolves the apparent inconsistency in Quranic verses that attribute death to different agents. It also provides a practical benefit for easing the pangs of death based on righteous acts and specific prayers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on attribution of tawaffi
Read the part of the passage that discusses the three attributions of death (tawaffi). Notice each attribution is supported by a Quranic verse.
Tawaffi means 'taking the soul'.
2
Distinguish the three attributions
Identify the three agents: the Angel of Death (verse 32:11), his helpers (verse 6:61), and Allah (verse 39:42).
Each attribution has a different reason.
3
Understand the reason for each attribution
The Angel of Death directly executes the taking; helpers extract the soul from the body; Allah is the Creator and true originator of the act.
No contradiction: it's levels of causation.
4
Read the benefit about easing death
Read the benefit (fa'ida) about righteous deeds, siwak, and a specific prayer on Friday night that eases death and its terrors.
This is a practical spiritual practice.
